Industry and Commerce 
The transition from a manufacturing-based economy to one that integrates technology and 
service firms are providing ne w job opportunities for the City and are diversifyi ng its tax base.  
The employment base of the City changed dramatically 10 years ago with the great recession and 
economic downturn, specifically in the automotive sector, which  once dominated employment in  
- 23 - 
this region.  Medical facilities and technology-related firms, as well as smaller manufacturers, 
distribution firms, insurance and mortgage processors are now r eshaping the economy of our 
community. 
Major investments in fiber communication and data networks that  exist within the City’s 
major corridors and highways, and abundant and redundant sources of clean power, water, sewer, 
and gas combined with a superb geographic location on I-70, make the City an attractive location 
for a multitude of industries.  Coupled with these natural attr ibutes, the City and its economic 
development partners have aggres sively added new industrial par ks and expanded shovel-ready 
opportunities for existing businesses and new companies. 
In 2018, the community adopted a new comprehensive land use and economic development 
plan named CONNECT Clark County.  This plan was created in collaboration with many different 
governmental entities and individuals with a stake and interest in ensuring that Springfield-Clark 
County reaches its full potential for residents, workers, businesses and visitors.  Over a 16-month 
process, dozens of stakeholder groups and thousands of individu als were engaged to ensure that 
the County would continue to evolve into a great place for many generations to come.  There were 
four overarching goals that emerged from the planning process as follows: 
 Strengthening Physical Character .  Support the development of d istinct and 
desirable places in which to liv e, work and play, and the conse rvation of land and 
the natural environment. 
 Advancing Prosperity.  Foster a robust and resilient economy th at promotes 
individual and community health , comfort and happiness through a range of 
employment options and a supportive climate for business and investment. 
 Enhancing Quality of Life.  Provide choice and opportunity in p laces, programs 
and services that serve housi ng, education, health, cultural, r ecreational and other 
needs of the community. 
 Improving Infrastructure.  Promo te transportation and utilities  that support 
development priorities and community well-being. 
Building from the countywide foundational plan, the Commission adopted a set of goals 
and objectives in 2018 that were aligned with the CONNECT Clark County plan but, focused more 
specifically on the following areas: 
 More Robust Economic Development.  Continue to attract jobs to the community 
and collaborate with community p artners and develop strategies that lead to the 
strengthening and growth of the I-70/South Limestone corridor, downtown and 
residential neighborhoods. 
 Improved Quality of Life.  Support new housing development and continue 
neighborhood code enforcement, reinstitute Safe Streets Task Force to help combat 
illegal drugs and crime, continue street improvements and conti nue investing in 
new and existing public infrastructure.  
- 24 - 
 More Vibrant Downtown.  Improve available parking and encourage  and promote 
downtown activities. 
 Improve Corridors.  Initiate preliminary design plans for the c ompleted South 
Limestone corridor study, create  a code enforcement strategy, c onstruct gateway 
signage on North Limestone or West Main Street using funding previously donated 
for this purpose. 
 Intentional Collaboration.  Continue to pursue combined 911 dispatching, identify 
Commissioner and/or other City re presentatives to participate w ith partner 
agencies/organizations. 
 Strong City Operations.  Improve communications within the City and between the 
City and its residents and partners, improve the quality of ser vices to residents by 
bringing all departments up to a uthorized strength, update tech nology 
infrastructure, work on staff development and develop a five-ye ar forecast for the 
City’s general fund operations. 
In an effort to increase the community’s attractiveness to technology and business service-
oriented companies, the City worked with its community partners , in particular the Chamber of 
Commerce, Community Improvement  Corporation, and the (Harry M.)  Turner Foundation, to 
complete the construction of a  215-acre technology park, Nexted ge Applied Research and 
Technology Park (“Nextedge”), centered between two strategic exits off of I-70. 
RELX Group/LexisNexis was the first occupant of Nextedge.  RELX  (formerly Reed 
Elsevier), a global leader in legal, news, and business information services, built a data facility in 
the City to expand its research and development efforts, host o nline products, and provide data 
management and data backup services.  The company employs 40 pe ople and has invested over 
$75 million in building, hardware and infrastructure systems since 2005. 
Speedway has an office within the park occupying a 55,000 square-foot, three-story office 
building.  Prior to Speedway locating at this site, the City assisted a private developer in securing 
a $3.1 million Ohio Job Ready Sites (“JRS”) grant to encourage the speculative construction of 
the research and development office space.  The office building is now owned by Speedway LLC, 
and is home to approximately 250 employees serving administrative, marketing and sales support 
for Speedway’s headquarters on the west side of Clark County, Ohio.  As one of Springfield-Clark 
County’s largest employers, Speedway has enjoyed tremendous growth, especially in the last five 
years.  In 2014, Speedway closed on its $2.8 billion acquisitio n of Hess Retail Holdings, which 
made Speedway the largest owned-and-operated convenience store chain in the U.S. by revenue 
and the second largest by number of stores. 
In 2018, Marathon Petroleum announced a $23.3 billion acquisiti on of Texas-based 
Andeavor, a marketing, logistics and refining company with operations stretching along the West 
Coast, which will have a significant impact on Speedway, adding as many as 1,100 new stores to 
the convenience store chain once the deal is finalized.  Speedw ay will grow to about 4,000 
locations from 2,800, and Speedway’s market territory will reac h from New Hampshire to 
California and from Alaska to Florida.  Locally, the project wi ll result in a $48.3 million project  
- 25 - 
to build a roughly 140,000 square -foot facility that would conn ect two existing buildings on its 
headquarters campus.  Speedway also plans to create at least 20 0 new, full-time corporate and 
office support jobs over the next three to four years as a result of its acquisition of Andeavor. 
Nextedge is also home to the Ohi o Unmanned Aerial Systems (“UAS ”) Center which 
operates as part of the Ohio Department of Transportation’s DriveOhio initiative given unmanned 
aircraft systems and autonomous/connected vehicles are parallel  technologies.  The State and the 
Air Force Research Laboratory (“AFRL”) in the city of Dayton have invested $5 million to install 
a ground-based sense-and-avoid radar system at the Springfield- Beckley Municipal Airport, 
potentially making the County one of the only research hubs of its kind in the nation.  The system 
gives the airport the unique capability of flying drones beyond the operator’s visual line of sight.  
The AFRL will use the system initially to test UAS, but univers ities and private companies will 
also be able to conduct testing at  the airport.  AFRL researche rs could begin test flights in early 
2019, pending approval from the Fed eral Aviation Administration.  Once the system is in place, 
the AFRL can use the airspace to train drone pilots and eventua lly test new technologies ranging 
from autopilot and sensor systems  to new concepts like swarming , in which multiple UAS 
cooperate to achieve a common mission.  The Air Force researche rs now often have to travel to 
various spots around the U.S. to find the needed airspace and range capabilities.  Within the City, 
AFRL can conduct the same research in less than a 20-minute dri ve time from their offices at 
Wright-Patterson Air Force Base (“Wright-Patterson AFB”). 
Complementing the City’s technology park is a new multi-strand fiber optic technology 
ring.  As a major inducement to those firms using high speed data networks to locate in Nextedge, 
a federal grant was procured for the design, equipment and cons truction of a fiber optic network 
to serve Nextedge, the Ohio National Guard missions, the Springfield-Beckley Municipal Airport 
and Wright-Patterson AFB.  The fir st phase of the fiber ring co nsisted of a 16-mile, 144-strand 
fiber network that looped around the City providing connectivity to universities, K-12, government 
offices and through Nextedge to multiple Points of Presence.  I n 2012, the second phase was 
completed, which connected the City loop with the Springfield-B eckley Municipal Airport, 
Airpark Ohio industrial park, Ohio Air National Guard Base, and Wright-Patterson AFB. 
Assurant Specialty Property, an insurance processing company, b egan operations in the 
City 20 years ago with just a few employees.  Today, the compan y is one of the City’s largest 
employers, with about 2,200 full-time employees in a beautiful campus setting that includes 
150,000 square feet of modern office facilities.  In 2012, the company embarked upon a major 
green initiative, investing ove r $7 million to install 6,200 so lar panel carports, covering most of 
their existing parking area. 
The City received a JRS grant of $5 million to assist with the $17 million renovation of the 
historic Bushnell office building in downtown Springfield.  The  construction began in 2009 and 
was completed in late 2011.  Sev enty-five thousand square feet of office space was renovated to 
meet LEED Certified Platinum, Smart Office Standards and is occ upied by multiple companies, 
including Code Blue, Clark Schaefer Hackett and many others. 
CodeBlue made the decision to loc ate in downtown Springfield in  2011.  The company 
offers expert, end-to-end claims management outsourcing solutio ns that yield the best cost and 
service outcome for insurance carriers.  CodeBlue is a division  of Eau Claire, Wisconsin  
- 26 - 
headquartered Insurance Claims Management Company.  The company  has approximately 150 
full-time employees and currently occupies 25,000 square feet w ithin the renovated Bushnell 
Building.  In early 2019, the company announced a potential exp ansion, wherein they expect to 
lease an additional 15,000 square feet to accommodate expected growth of its business, which will 
result in the hiring of 169 new employees over three years.  Co deBlue acts as a liaison between 
homeowners, insurance companies and independent contractors.  I nsurance firms contract with 
CodeBlue to dispatch contractors to sites where water damage has occurred. 
In 2014, Clark Schaefer Hackett signed a lease for 17,000 squar e feet in the historic 
Bushnell Building in downtown Springfield.  The company has app roximately 60 employees 
operating a full-service accounting firm. 
Early in 2004, both Community Hospital and Mercy Health Partner s signed a hospital 
merger agreement.  The new entity, known as Community Mercy Hea lth Partners (“CMHP”), 
became the City’s largest employer with total employment of app roximately 2,600.  As a part of 
the merger agreement, the two entities agreed that a new region al medical facility would be 
constructed.  City staff along with CMHP representatives worked to identify the most appropriate 
site for the new regional medical facility construction project .  A 120-acre area known as the 
Southwest Downtown Urban Renewal Area was designated for redevelopment.  A 50-acre portion 
of the area was identified as the regional medical facility cor e development area, with at least 
another 20 acres planned for medical ancillary uses.  In May 20 06, the formal redevelopment 
agreement was authorized and enacted.  Ground was broken at the site of the new medical facility 
in October 2008.  Construction of the new facility took three years and the facility officially opened 
in November 2011.  The road realignments, new streetscape and b oulevard designs encouraged 
and enhanced pedestrian flow int o the downtown mixed-use distri cts and the project acted as a 
much-needed catalyst for development for the core downtown area. 
In conjunction with the opening o f the hospital, the Community Health Foundation 
(“CHF”) celebrated the opening of the CHF Health Service Pavili on where patients can receive 
reproductive care, such as birth control and sterilization serv ices.  The 11,000 square-foot, 
$3.4 million pavilion building, which is owned by CHF and is part of the new Springfield Regional 
Medical Center, consists of operating rooms and exam rooms, administrative offices and space for 
leasing. 
The construction of the $275 million Springfield Regional Medic al Center was a catalyst 
for downtown development.  While the construction of the main h ospital was underway, an 
investment group of surgeons announced plans to construct a $15 million surgical hospital, named 
Ohio Valley Medical Center at the former Greenawalt-Trenor site .  The facility opened in 2009 
and was later renamed Ohio Valley Surgical Hospital (“OVSH”); t he practice specializes in 
elective outpatient and short stay surgeries. 
In 2012, OVSH completed construction of a $5 million, 31,000 square-foot medical office 
building to accommodate physicians’ offices, imaging, physical therapy, pre-admission testing and 
other tenants.  OVSH expanded again in 2015 with the purchase o f a former Robertson Can 
industrial property.  OVSH demolished and cleared the site to add parking in support of the main 
campus and to hold the two-acre site for future expansion.  
- 27 - 
A major focus of the City’s economic development staff is on th e revitalization of the 
downtown and remediation and re development of old industrial si tes (Brownfields).  Significant 
progress was made over the past decade in addressing the City’s  former industrial sites.  Beyond 
the hospital site, four additional downtown sites were cleared and readied for development.  The 
former Bayley manufacturing site was redeveloped into the Sprin gfield Regional Cancer Center; 
the former Greenawalt-Trenor property was redeveloped into a st ate-of-the-art medical campus, 
which is home to OVSH; the former Haucke building and surrounding properties were cleared and 
remediated to facilitate the construction of a new community ic e rink and the Memorial Hall 
building was demolished to make way for new commercial developm ent across from the main 
campus of the hospital. 
A center city park named National Road Commons was added in the  center of downtown 
in 2011.  This section is the first phase of a wedge-shaped par k that is envisioned to span from 
Buck Creek to the Heritage Center and features a tiered grassy area and a plaza, as well as a pergola 
and fountains.  The relocated Madonna of the Trail statue serves as a cornerstone to the downtown 
park.  The recreational park setting serves as a gathering spac e for downtown functions but also 
acts as an economic development anchor to encourage redevelopment of sites adjacent to the park.  
A state grant and private donors paid the project costs of $2.5 million. 
In 2011, Clark State Community Co llege completed construction o f the $5.6 million 
Hollenbeck Bayley Creative Arts and Conference Center, adjacent  to the Kuss Auditorium in 
downtown.  The 27,000 square-foot conference center features a grand hall which accommodates 
up to 500 people for conferences, meetings or performances, while it also has classrooms, labs and 
an art studio.   
The National Trail Parks and R ecreation District opened an $8.5  million downtown ice 
arena in 2013.  The ice arena was the final project to be completed as part of the $17 million capital 
campaign that also included Carleton Davidson Stadium, Splash Z one Aquatic Center and 
improvements to Veterans Park Amphitheater and other smaller parks around the county.  The ice 
rink is managed by the Columbus-based Chiller LLC, which is own ed by the Columbus Blue 
Jackets and operates five ice rinks in central Ohio. 
In 2016, United Senior Services (“USS”) completed its $6 million renovation to the former 
Fraternal Order of Eagles faci lity.  The USS center relocated t o the new space as it is roughly 
double the size of the previous USS center on South Fountain Avenue, due to the growing demand 
for elderly-related services in the community.  The new facilit y features a saltwater aquatic pool, 
a dance floor and also features pieces of the historic Memorial  Hall, including the wood ticket 
booth and original stained-glass windows that fill one of the conference rooms. 
A new microbrewery was opened in  downtown during the summer of 2016.  The project 
included a $1.3 million renovation of the former Metallic Caske t manufacturing facility.  The 
brewery features a tap room and outdoor patio and hosts food trucks and many local functions. 
Wittenberg University broke ground in March 2017 on a $40 million indoor athletic facility 
that they hope will help with student recruitment and facilitat e more connections with the 
Springfield community.  Wittenberg projects construction comple tion by 2019.  Among the 
features of the new 125,000 square-foot complex will be an indoor 100-yard artificial turf surface;  
- 28 - 
a six-lane, 300-meter indoor track; a 7,000 square-foot weight room; a sport performance, strength 
training and wellness center; te chnology-enabled classroom spac e; updated locker rooms; and 
modern court surfaces for tennis, volleyball and basketball.  Wittenberg is a major local employer 
with a total of more than 350 employees and an estimated $70 mi llion economic impact on the 
Springfield community. 
The City owned the former USS property in downtown Springfield and considered several 
redevelopment options once USS decided to relocate to a new facility.  In 2018, the City sold the 
building to SpringForward, a nonprofit organization with the mi ssion of boosting investment in 
downtown Springfield.  In turn, SpringForward announced plans t o collaborate with COhatch, a 
co-working lifestyle company based in Worthington, Ohio, to ope n a year-round artisan’s 
marketplace, shared kitchen, food hall, and co-working, lifesty le and event space for local 
entrepreneurs, businesses and i ndividuals.  The shared kitchen will offer a range of options for 
entrepreneurs, startups and food trucks requiring modern equipment and a shared cost model.  The 
new food hall will be an ideal setting for local farmers, healt hy eateries, caterers, experiential 
dinners and novelty restaurateurs.  The facility is expected to open in 2019.  
A local investor and executive chef announced plans in early 2019 to open a new sit-down 
restaurant in downtown Springfield to be located in the historic Bushnell building.  The restaurant 
will be named Stella Bleu and seat as many as 85 customers inside, with a separate private dining 
space that can seat approximately 50 guests.  In addition, one of the restaurant’s features will be 
an outdoor patio that can seat an additional 75 to 100 guests as it looks out over Fountain Avenue. 
Plans for a three-story parking garage with approximately 275 to 325 parking spaces at the 
corner of Fountain Avenue and Columbia Street moved ahead in la te 2018.  The Commission 
approved $50,000 for design and construction concepts, and cons truction is expected to begin in 
spring 2019.  The roughly $7 million downtown parking garage ha s been discussed for several 
years as a way to provide additional parking downtown as the City works to attract more retail and 
business to the City center.  The site at the corner of Fountain Avenue and Columbia Street is now 
a City-owned parking lot.  The garage plan includes adding additional retail space along the street 
level on Fountain Avenue.  Multiple funding sources came together in support of the project with 
the County contributing $1 million in funding for the project, the State has committed $2.55 
million of state capital grants, and the City pledged to contri bute the site and up to $3 million for 
the project.  
Retail development begun on North Fountain Avenue in 2016 continues to flourish.  That 
year, Winans Chocolates and Coffees opened its doors.  The Piqu a-based business began 
franchising in 2003 and has locations at German Village in Columbus, the cities of Maineville and 
Beavercreek and the Dayton Mall.  Le Torte Dolce, a locally-own ed bakery, opened next door to 
Winans in 2017, and those owners then opened Salato Deli in an adjoining space in 2018. 
The Springfield area’s logistic s and distribution industry empl oys thousands of workers.  
Among the leading companies are the Eby-Brown Company, a conven ience store supplier and 
wholesale food distributor of tobacco, candy and convenience products with about 450 employees.  
Eby-Brown is the exclusive supplier to Speedway and is expected  to see tremendous growth as a 
result of Speedway’s acquisition strategy.  
- 29 - 
Reiter Dairy continues to grow through the consolidation of Dean Foods related facilities.  
In 2011, Dean Foods closed a plant in Kentucky and consolidated  production at the Springfield 
location.  The company has invested millions of dollars in plant upgrades and milk storage, as well 
as the acquisition of property adjacent to its facilities on Co mmerce Circle.  The company 
demolished the former International Steel Wool building and improved the lot for additional trailer 
and employee parking. 
Lineage Logistics owns and ope rates a 127,000 squa re-foot refrigerated warehouse and 
distribution center for the exclusive support of Bob Evans Farm s Inc. (“Bob Evans”).  Lineage 
Logistics is a warehousing and logistics partner committed to delivering sophisticated, customized 
and dependable cold chain solutions to leading food, retail, agriculture and distribution companies. 
In 2012, Bob Evans invested nearly $3.5 million in the construc tion of a truck service 
facility, adjacent to the distri bution center.  The transportat ion center houses 35 full-time 
employees and 80 full-time drivers. 
US Xpress Enterprises, Inc. (“US Xpress”) invested nearly $4 mi llion in acquiring and 
improving a truck terminal facility in 2009.  US Xpress provides dedicated, regional, and expedited 
team truckload services throughout North America, with regional  capabilities in the West, 
Midwest and Southeastern United States.  The company is one of the largest providers of expedited 
and time-definite services in the truckload industry.  Locally,  US Xpress has a total of 435 
employees, including 370 drivers and 65 office/maintenance employees. 
Manufacturers have shown a stead y increase in equipment investm ents and in hiring.  
Manufacturers involved in aerospace, automotive (autos and larg e trucks), and the production of 
materials handling equipment have seen solid growth. 
JM Consolidated Industries est ablished a 60,000 square-foot fac ility located within 
Springfield Industrial Park.  The owner of the building renovated the existing office and added an 
additional 3,000 square feet to accommodate the larger administrative component.  The expansion 
project included 50 new full-time positions and retained anothe r 46 existing full-time positions.  
JM Consolidated Industries manufactures window regulators for the heavy truck industry. 
Navistar, Inc., another major employer in the County, took steps in 2015 to becoming more 
stable after reaching a new four-year agreement with its union members.  The company had as few 
as 300 workers in the City as recently as 2010, but rebounded a nd has continued to boost 
employment.  Also in 2015, Navistar struck an agreement with General Motors to build medium-
duty trucks in the City, along with a pledge to add at least 30 0 jobs and a more than $12 million 
investment for that work through 2018, bringing the workforce t o 600.  An additional 300 jobs 
were added at Navistar in 2016 and a second deal with General M otors to build a cutaway model 
of General Motors’ G Van beginning in 2017.  Navistar reopened a manufacturing line in the City 
that had been closed since approximately 2001, to build the G Vans.  The agreement was expected 
to create as much as $10.4 million in new payroll.  In 2017, th e truck maker announced that 
Volkswagen would purchase a roughly 17-percent stake in the com pany and agreed to a 
partnership with Navistar to produce a new line of medium duty electric semi-trucks.  
- 30 - 
Pentaflex, a Springfield manufacturing firm, announced in 2014 that it would hire 40 new 
workers over three years and add 20,000 square feet to its plant as part of a $4 million expansion, 
after seeing business steadily grow over the years.  The expansion represents a roughly $8 million 
investment overall when combined with two servo press lines tha t were installed in 2014.  This 
expansion was the company’s most significant since 2000, when it moved from its previous facility 
on East Columbia Street to its current location in the Prime Ohio Corporate Park. 
In 2014, Dole Fresh Vegetables completed a $9 million expansion that led to 138 new jobs 
at its Springfield plant.  The deal generated an additional $3. 2 million in annual payroll, retained 
approximately 600 jobs and $17.4 million in annual payroll.  Th e company added three new 
packaging lines and one processing line to produce Dole’s tende r-leaf packaged vegetable 
products, which include spinach, spring mix and baby lettuce.  
Heroux-Devtek Inc. (HDI), a Canadian manufacturing company that  produces aerospace 
and industrial products, purchased Eagle Tool and Machine Co. in the City in 2010, establishing a 
subsidiary named HDI Landing Gear USA.   In 2014, the company landed a major contract to 
become a Tier 1 supplier to Boeing to produce landing gear for Boeing’s jewel aircraft, the 777 
and 777X.  Deliveries began in 2017, and the contract includes an option to extend through 2028.  
Under the agreement, the company will supply complete landing g ear systems for Boeing, 
including the main and nose landing gear, as well as the nose gear drag strut.  HDI Landing Gear 
USA estimated an investment of about $24.5 million in new equipment and in its facility. 
In late 2014, local officials cel ebrated the opening of PrimeOh io II, an industrial park 
containing 200 acres, located on Ridge Road along I-70 across from the Clark County Fairgrounds.  
The development received a $150,000 JRS grant to extend road ac cess and improve natural gas 
line capacity to the site.  In all, PrimeOhio II is a $7.5 million development that will provide shovel-
ready land for industries looking to expand or locate their ope rations within the I-70/I-75/I-71 
corridor. 
Topre, a Japanese auto supplier, made its first move into the C ity-owned Champion City 
Business Park in December 2016, when the Japanese auto parts fi rm announced plans to invest 
$10 million in a new 20,000 square-f oot stamping facility that would create 20 jobs.  In 
March 2017, Topre increased its investment to $55 million in a 177,000 square-foot facility, 
adding 85 jobs after acquiring additional work supplying manufa cturers like Honda and Toyota.  
A few months later, the company announced another expansion, with plans to add another 138,000 
square feet, creating 204 more jobs and investing $73 million.  In total, the company’s investment 
represents approximately $130 million, with a projected total workforce of nearly 300 employees.  
Once all phases are complete, Topre’s plant will fill the rough ly 30-acre industrial site that once 
housed an International Harvester/Navistar factory until it closed in 2002.  The business park was 
completed in 2013 after several years of cleani ng up industrial  contaminants and improving 
infrastructure.  
In late 2017, Silfex, a high-tech manufacturing firm, purchased the former Thirty-One Gifts 
facility on Titus Road, announced  a project that would create 4 00 jobs with investment of $223 
million.  The company projected annual payroll of approximately  $20.7 million.  Pay will vary 
depending on the job duties, but will average about $53,000 a year, including benefits.  The high-
tech manufacturing firm chose to expand in the City after narrowing the field from about 50 other  
- 31 - 
sites.  Silfex was founded in Preble County, Ohio and builds si licon components for several 
industries, including everything from computer processors to ce ll phones to cars.  The firm also 
grows silicon crystals at its e xisting plant in Preble County, Ohio and will perform similar 
functions in the City.  The company is on track to begin produc tion at the Titus Road plant in the 
first quarter of 2019.  A second phase, in which the company ramps up its silicon crystal growing 
operation, will likely begin operation in 2020. 
The City expanded Airpark Ohio industrial park in 2011 by platting an additional 47 acres, 
creating five industrial lots ranging from six to 12 acres.  In  addition, the City was awarded two 
grants from the State (ODOT/ODOD) to aid in the extension of ut ilities to serve the Bob Evans 
Farms transportation center.  In addition, the City acquired 40  adjoining acres to Airpark Ohio to 
allow for continued growth poten tial for the industrial park.  In August 2011, the City obtained 
approval for the rezoning of this land from agricultural to industrial usage in order to accommodate 
companies looking for larger sites to develop. 
Meva Formwork Systems Inc. (“Meva”) completed construction of t he company’s North 
American headquarters and distribution facility in 2007 and in 2012, announced a $2.5 million 
expansion to include the acquisition of six additional acres of  property at Airpark Ohio.  The 
company retained 35 full-time employees and created 15 new full-time positions as a result of the 
expansion. 
Spectra Jet Inc., which services, inspects, and repairs Lear jets, completed construction of 
a 24,000 square foot hangar in 2007 at the Springfield-Beckley Municipal Airport.  The business 
has grown steadily and the company announced the second phase ( expansion) of its hangar in 
2017.  The proposed $3.3 million expansion includes approximate ly 45,000 square feet, 
encompassing a two-story executive office complex and an additi onal 24,000 square-foot hangar 
and shop areas. 
In 2014, Ali-Gator Air amended its lease with the City to inclu de an additional 10,000 
square feet of property to expand its aircraft parking apron an d also to allow for future hangar 
expansion.  In 2016, Ali-Gator Air added an office and passenge r waiting area to its hangar for a 
total investment of $240,000. 
In 2008, the City leased an aircraft hangar at the Springfield-Beckley Municipal Airport to 
SelectTech Services Corporation (“SelectTeach”) related to the establishment of  its Advanced 
Manufacturing Facility.  Selec tTech uses the 17,000 square-foot  hangar to manufacture airborne 
mobile communications and related systems that can be used acro ss a variety of military aircraft.  
Flight line access allows the company to efficiently load and t est the modules.  SelectTech is 
heavily involved in the procurement of UAV/UAS related contract s.  The company’s customers 
include Wright-Patterson AFB, AFR and many others related to in telligence, surveillance and 
reconnaissance type activities.  The City continues to work wit h SelectTech officials to facilitate 
building improvements on the existing hangar and jointly market  the airport/airpark to potential 
suppliers of SelectTech and/or AFRL. 
The U.S. Air Force and the State have invested over $5 million in research and equipment 
in hopes the Springfield-Beckley Municipal Airport will be appr oved by the Federal Aviation 
Administration for engineers to fly unmanned aircraft beyond visual line of sight, something that  
- 32 - 
cannot be done anywhere else in the nation right now.  Federal Aviation Administration regulations 
require that aircraft be able to see and avoid other aircraft.  Remotely piloted aircraft, such as 
unmanned drones, must have ground observers or chase aircraft t o make sure they steer clear of 
other vehicles like ultralights, gliders, hot air balloons and crop dusters — all of which may not be 
easily tracked by air traffic controllers.  The new system under development by AFRL at Wright-
Patterson AFB would use ground sensors (Air Traffic Control Radar Data) to monitor the airspace 
for remotely piloted aircraft.  Unmanned aerial systems are inc reasingly used for precision 
agriculture, search and rescue, law enforcement and environment al functions.  The ground-based 
sensor system will be the first such system tested in a high-traffic area.  The Ohio UAS Test Center, 
based in the City, will operate the equipment.  The system will allow for UAS flight testing in the 
State and it could attract companies and investment to the Springfield area.  The new technology 
could lead to changes in national security and defense operations on top of commercial enterprises 
using unmanned aerial vehicles to deliver goods to people’s doorsteps.

Utilities; Public Safety and Services 
Water service within the City is provided by the City water system and is purchased directly 
by the consumers.  Sewage collection and disposal is provided b y the City owned and operated 
sewage and water pollution control system.  Electricity distrib ution is provided through an 
aggregation program administered by Northeastern Ohio Public Energy Council (NOPEC), natural 
gas is supplied by Columbia Gas of Ohio, Inc. and local telepho ne service is provided by 
Ameritech.  Solid waste collection is contracted by property ow ners, provided by various 
companies, and is sent to various landfills. 
Community and Economic Development 
To accommodate expanding industry, new and growing businesses a nd overall economic 
growth, new housing and development of existing housing stock have been a focus in recent years 
and great progress was made in 2018 and into 2019.   
In December 2018, City officials reached a redevelopment agreem ent with Charles V. 
Simms Development Corporation (“Simms”) to build 34 townhomes a t a downtown site located  
- 37 - 
between West Main and West Columbia streets.  The proposed development will include 34 three-
story units with built-in garages with access from Center Stree t.  Each home will be about 1,300 
square feet with prices starting around $200,000.  The City is responsible for preparing the site for 
construction and transferring the site to Simms for the constru ction of the townhomes.  Simms 
projected construction to begin in late spring 2019.  Simms is a Dayton-based company founded 
in 1957.  The company specializes in maintenance communities an d has built 115 townhomes in 
the city of Dayton similar to those planned for downtown. 
City officials reached a deal wit h a national developer to buil d a 38-acre housing 
development on property south of the Tuttle Road Walmart along with an additional 15-acre tract 
to the east of the Walmart.  The project will include more than 230 new houses built in four separate 
phases.  The housing development will be named Bridgewater with  the first expected to begin in 
2019 which will include 71 new homes. 
The Community Gardens senior apartments, an 86-unit affordable senior housing 
development planned on the 11.5-acre site at the corner of High Street and Burnett Road, had the 
grand opening of its first phase in 2018.  Phase II of the project moved forward in 2019 when the 
Commission approved the Neighborh ood Housing Partners’ request to apply for funding.  Other 
partners in the project include the Ohio Housing Finance Agency , the Ohio Capital Corporation 
for Housing, NeighborWorks America, River Hills Bank and Kapp Construction. 
A $15.3 million renovation of the Edward Wren Co. Building will be the site of 28 market-
rate apartments with ground floor restaurant space.  The property is owned by the Wren Building 
Partners, which was set up by the Turner Foundation.  The prope rty was built in 1921 as a 
department store and bank in the heart of downtown. 
Johnson Flats is the location of loft apartments next to four additional apartments recently 
renovated by the Turner Foundation.  A 1,000 square-foot apartment rents for $1,200 a month and 
a smaller 951 square-foot apartment rents for $1,000 per month. 
In 2015, Deitzel Apartments was renovated to include granite countertops, hardwood floors 
and crown molding.  Each apartment has an open floor plan and i ncludes between 600 and 900 
square feet of space. 
The City has made concerted efforts to implement proactive ince ntives to encourage 
growth within the existing housing stock, as well as find strategic solutions to continue new growth 
in the future.  The City commissioned a housing study to identify opportunities citywide that will 
assist in encouraging investment and attract businesses and residents to the City. 
In 2017, the Commission approved offering 100 percent tax-relie f in community 
reinvestment areas throughout the  City on new and renovated res idential properties for up to 15 
years.  The City is currently making efforts to expand that program in 2019. 
The City has also taken advantage of Opportunity Zones to encou rage growth and 
investment citywide.  The Opportunity Zones were established as  part of the 2017 Tax Cuts and 
Jobs Act, which allowed states to identify low-income, high-poverty Census tracts for the program.  
The program will provide federal tax advantages to investors wh o seek to reduce or defer their  
- 38 - 
capital gains tax burden and who invest in funds that funnel mo ney into economically distressed 
areas. 
Joint Economic Development District 
A Joint Economic Development District (“JEDD”) is an agreement between two parties:  a 
township and a city or village that share commercial and industrial development benefits and 
responsibilities.  The law authorizing JEDD’s went into effect December 14, 1992, and are unique 
to the State.  The City and Green Township, Ohio were the first  to enter into an agreement with 
the signing of the JEDD contract dated September 7, 1993.  The statutory approval process was 
completed and the JEDD contract went into effect December 1993. 
When the JEDD District was initially implemented, it encompassed 1,359 acres of property 
owned by the City, mainly making up the land of the Springfield -Beckley Municipal Airport and 
the Airpark Ohio Industrial Park.  Historically, a portion of this land was leased to the Department 
of Defense in support of the Ohio Air National Guard. 
Since that time, the City has acquired additional property to support the growth of the Air 
National Guard Base, the realignment of S.R. 794, protection of encroachment around the airport 
and for the future growth of the industrial park.  Currently, the JEDD encompasses 1,630 acres.  
There are six companies served through the industrial park whic h include Ali-Air, Bob Evans 
Transportation Center, Lineage Logistics, MEVA Formworks, Spect ra Jet and United Fiberglass 
of America.  There are four additional companies, SelectTech Ge o Spatial – Advanced 
Manufacturing Facility, Matrix Corporation, Champion City Flight School and S-Jet, at the airport.  
The Ohio Air National Guard and Army National Guard missions have evolved over the years but 
have grown a stronger presence in the Department of Defense’s global strategy.  
FINANCIAL MATTERS 
Introduction 
The City’s fiscal year corresponds with the calendar year. 
The main sources of City revenue have been and are property taxes, income taxes and State 
distributions, as described below. 
The responsibilities for the majo r financial functions of the C ity are divided among the 
Finance Director (the “Fiscal Officer”), the Treasurer, the City Manager and the Commission. 
Other important financial function s include general financial r ecommendations and 
planning by the Fiscal Officer, budget preparation by the City Manager with the assistance of the 
Fiscal Officer, the City’s fiscal and chief accounting officer.   In  th is  ro l e ,  th a t  o f f ic e r’s  d u tie s  
include keeping the books and accurate statements of all money received and expended and of all 
taxes and assessments; at the end of each Fiscal Year, or more often if requested by the 
Commission, to examine all accounts of City officers and depart ments; and ensuring that the 
amount set aside for any appropria tion is not overdrawn, or the  amount appropriated for any one 
item of expense is not drawn upon for any other purpose, or a v oucher is only paid if sufficient  
- 39 - 
funds are in the City treasury to the credit of the fund on which the voucher is drawn.  The Treasurer 
is responsible for receiving, maintaining custody of and disbursing all City funds. 
The Fiscal Officer has charge of the financial affairs of the City, including the keeping and 
supervision of all City accounts and, except that the Treasurer  has responsibility for the custody 
and disbursements of all City funds and moneys.  The Fiscal Officer is appointed by the Mayor. 
For property taxation purposes, assessment of real property is by the County Auditor 
subject to supervision by the State Tax Commissioner, and assessment of public utility and tangible 
personal property is by the Stat e Tax Commissioner.  Property t axes and assessments are billed 
and collected by County officials. 
Budgeting, Property Tax Levy and Appropriations Procedures 
Detailed provisions for budgeting, property tax levies and appr opriations are made in the 
Revised Code, including a requirement that the City levy a prop erty tax in a sufficient amount, 
with any other money available for the purpose, to pay the debt charges on securities payable from 
property taxes. 
The law requires generally that a subdivision prepare, and then adopt after a public hearing, 
a tax budget approximately six months before the start of the n ext fiscal year.  The tax budget is 
then presented for review by the county budget commission, whic h is comprised of the county 
auditor, treasurer and prosecuting attorney.  A county budget commission may however, waive the 
requirement for a tax budget and require an alternative form of more limited information required 
by the commission to perform its duties.  The Clark County Budg et Commission has not waived 
the requirement of a tax budget from the City and other subdivisions in the County. 
The county budget commission then determines and approves levie s for debt charges 
outside and inside the ten-mill limitation.  The Revised Code provides that “if any debt charge is 
omitted from the budget, the commission shall include it therein.” 
The county budget commission then certifies to each subdivision  its action on the tax 
budget together with the estimate by the County Auditor of the tax rates outside and inside the 
ten-mill limitation.  Thereafter, and before the end of the then Fiscal Year, the taxing authority (the 
Commission in the case of the City) approves the tax levies and certifies them to the county auditor.  
The approved and certified tax rates are then reflected in the tax bills sent to property owners.  Real 
property taxes are payable in two equal installments, the first usually by February and the second 
in July. 
Under the Charter, the City Man ager is charged with the respons ibility of preparing the 
estimates of revenues and expenditures for the ensuing fiscal ( calendar) year on or before 
November 1 of each year.  After reviewing the City Manager’s recommendations, the Commission 
holds a public hearing on the proposed appropriations budget.  The Commission typically adopts 
the Appropriations Ordinance prior to December 31 for the ensui ng fiscal year.  Although called 
“permanent”, the annual appropriation measure may be, and often  is, amended during the Fiscal 
Year.  Annual appropriations may not exceed the County Budget Commission’s official estimates 
of resources, and the County Aud itor must certify that the City ’s appropriation measures do not 
appropriate money in excess of the amounts set forth in those estimates.  
- 40 - 
Financial Reports and Audits 
The City maintains its accounts, appropriations and other fiscal records in accordance with 
the procedures established and prescribed by the Ohio Auditor of State (the “State Auditor”).  The 
State Auditor is charged by law with the responsibility of inspecting and supervising the accounts 
and records of each taxing subdivision and most public agencies and institutions. 
City receipts and expenditures are compiled on a cash basis, pu rsuant to accounting 
procedures prescribed by the Sta te Auditor that are generally a pplicable to all Ohio political 
subdivisions.  The records of these cash receipts and expenditu res are converted annually for 
reporting purposes to a modified accrual basis of accounting for governmental funds and an accrual 
basis for proprietary funds.  These accounting procedures confo rm to generally accepted 
accounting principles as prescribed by the Governmental Account ing Standards Board (GASB).  
Those principles, among other things, provide for a modified ac crual basis of accounting for the 
general fund, all special revenue funds, and the debt service (bond retirement) fund and for a full 
accrual basis of accounting for al l other funds, and for the pr eparation for each fund of balance 
sheets, statements of revenues and expenditures, and statements showing changes in fund balances. 
The City has issued a Comprehensive Annual Financial Report (“C AFR”), including 
General Purpose and Basic Financ ial Statements (“Basic Financia l Statements”), for each of the 
Fiscal Years 1985 through 2017.  The CAFRs through Fiscal Year 2017 were awarded the 
Government Finance Officers Association’s Certificate of Achiev ement for Excellence in 
Financial Reporting, which is awarded to those governmental reporting agencies that comply with 
the GFOA reporting standards. 
Audits are made by the State Auditor, or CPAs at the direction of that officer, pursuant to 
Ohio law.  No other independent examination or audit of the City’s financial records is made. 
The most recent audit (including compliance audit) of the City’ s accounts by the State 
Auditor was completed through Fiscal Year 2017.  The Basic Financial Statements of the City for 
Fiscal Year 2017 are set forth as Appendix C; they have been audited by Clark Schaefer Hackett 
& Co. independent auditors, as st ated in their report appearing  in those statements.  No material 
findings, citations or items for adjustment, or material weaknesses in internal controls, were noted 
as part of the audit. 
Annual financial reports are prepared by the City, and are filed as required by law with the 
State Auditor after the close of each Fiscal Year. 
See Appendix A for an unaudited comparative cash-basis summary, prepared by the City 
of General Fund receipts and expenditures for the last five Fis cal Years and budgeted for Fiscal 
Year 2019.  All funds receipts and expenditures for the two pri or Fiscal Years are set forth in 
Appendix B.  See Appendix C for the audited Basic Financial Statements for Fiscal Year 201 7 
including the audit letter/independent accountant’s report. 
The audited financial statements  are public records, no consent  to their inclusion is 
required, and no bring-down procedures have been undertaken by the private audit firm since their 
date  
- 41 - 
Deposits and Investments 
Deposits and investments of City funds are governed by the Unif orm Depository Law 
(Chapter 135 of the Revised Code ) applicable to all subdivision s.  The Finance Director is 
responsible for those investments and deposits.  Under recent and current practices, and the City’s 
adopted investment policy, in addition to deposits evidenced by  interest-bearing certificates of 
deposit, investments are made in the State Treasurer’s subdivis ion investment pool, STAR Ohio, 
and federal or federal agency securities, and certain of the Ci ty’s own bonds and notes.  See also 
Notes to the Fiscal Year 2017 Basic Financial Statements in Appendix C. 
The City from time to time also invests in STAR Ohio (State Tre asury Asset Reserve) 
which is an investment pool managed by the Ohio Treasurer of St ate.  STAR Ohio is similar in 
concept to a registered investment company issuing redeemable s ecurities, commonly called a 
“money market mutual fund.”  A treasurer, governing board or in vestment authority of a 
subdivision may deposit public money of the subdivision with the Treasurer of State.  Subdivision 
is defined in Section 135.5(E)(2) of the Revised Code as any county, municipal corporation, school 
district, township, municipal or school district sinking fund, special taxing or assessment district 
and other district or local authority electing or appointing a treasurer.  The Treasurer of State can 
invest the public money deposited in STAR Ohio in the same types of instruments as are provided 
for the investment of interim money of the State. 
Investments in stripped principal or interest obligations, reverse repurchase agreements and 
derivatives are prohibited.  The  issuance of taxable notes for the purpose of arbitrage, the use of 
leverage and short selling are also prohibited.  Investments ma y only be made through specified 
dealers and institutions.

Interest Rate Risk 
As a means of limiting its exposure to fair value losses caused  by rising interest rates, the 
City’s investment policy requires that, to the extent possible,  investments will match anticipated 
cash flow requirements.  Unless matched to a specific cash flow requirement, the Finance Director 
will directly invest in securities as follows: not more than 20 percent of the value (computed at the 
time the investment is made) of investments to mature or be redeemable within five years from the 
date of purchase and the balance of all investments to mature o r be redeemable within five years 
from the date of purchase. 
Credit Risk 
The City’s investment policy limits investments to those authorized by State statute which 
restricts investments to those that are highly rated or backed by the enterprises of the United States 
Government.  Ohio law requires that STAR Ohio maintain the high est rating provided by at least 
one nationally recognized standard rating service. 
Concentration of Credit Risk 
The City places no limit on the amount it may invest in any one  issuer.  The percentage 
that each investment represents of total investments is listed in the table above. 
The State of Ohio has recently reduced the City’s allocation of  the Local Government 
Funds.  This reduction is not an ticipated to materially affect City finances, as it accounts for less 
than 1% of total general fund revenues.  The State of Ohio has also eliminated the estate tax 
revenue for local governments.  Although City revenues from this source could be material in any 
specific year, it has been extremely unpredictable and has vari ed dramatically from year to year.  
As a result, the City has not typically included estimates for such receipts in its budgeting process.

through 2018 and budgeted for 2019 are shown in Appendix A. 
GENERAL FUND 
The General Fund is the City’s main operating fund, from which most expenditures are 
paid and into which most revenues are deposited.  The General F und receives money from many 
sources, but primarily from ad valorem property taxes and incom e taxes levied by the City and 
State local government distributions.  Appendices A and B provide further information regarding 
other revenue sources for the General Fund and other City funds.

Pursuant to statutory requirements for sexennial reappraisals, the County Auditor adjusted 
the true value of taxable real property to reflect current fair  market values.  These adjustments 
were first reflected in the 2017 duplicate (collection year 201 8) and in the ad valorem taxes 
distributed to the City in 2018 and thereafter.  The County Auditor is required to adjust (but without 
individual appraisal of propert ies except in the sexennial reap praisal), and has adjusted, taxable 
real property value triennially to reflect true values.  
The “assessed valuation” of real property is fixed at 35% of tr ue value and is determined 
pursuant to rules of the State Tax Commissioner.  An exception is that real property devoted 
exclusively to agricultural use is to be assessed at not more than 35% of its current agricultural use 
value.  Real property devoted exclusively to forestry or timber growing is taxed at 50% of the local 
tax rate upon its assessed value.  
- 45 - 
Public utility tangible personal property (with some exceptions ) is currently assessed 
(depending on the type of property) from 25% to 88% of true value.  Effective for collection year 
2002, the assessed valuation of electric utility production equipment was reduced from 100% and 
natural gas utility property from 88% of true value, both to 25% of true value. 
The taxation of all tangible personal property used in general businesses (excluding certain 
public utility tangible personal property) was phased out over tax years 2006 to 2009.  Previously, 
machinery and equipment and furniture and fixtures were general ly taxed at 25% of true value, 
and inventory was taxed at 23%.  The taxation of all tangible personal property used by telephone, 
telegraph or interexchange telecommunications companies (“telecommunications property”) was 
phased out over tax years 2007 to 2011.  Previously, telecommun ications property was taxed at 
25% or 46% of true value (depending on the type of equipment an d when it was placed into 
service). 
As a result of the phase-out of taxation of tangible personal p roperty used in general 
business (excluding certain pub lic utility property) and tangib le personal property used by 
telephone, telegraph or interexchange telecommunications companies, and the 2002 reduction of 
the percentages of true value of electric utility production eq uipment and natural gas utility 
property assessed for taxation, as  described above, eligible lo cal governments have received 
reimbursement payments from the State to account for the loss of property tax revenue.  The City 
no longer receives reimbursement from the State relating to los t property revenue from operating 
levies. 
As indicated herein, the General Assembly has from time to time  exercised its power to 
revise the laws applicable to th e determination of assessed val uation of taxable property and the 
amount of receipts to be produced by ad valorem taxes levied on  that property and may continue 
to make similar revisions. 
Ohio law grants tax credits to offset increases in taxes result ing from increases in the true 
value of real property.  Legislation classifies real property as between residential and agricultural 
property and all other real prope rty, and provides for tax redu ction factors to be separately 
computed for and applied to each class. 
These tax credits apply only to certain voted levies on real property, and they do not apply 
to unvoted levies, or to voted levies to provide a specified dollar amount or to pay debt charges on 
general obligation debt such as the Bonds.  These credits are d iscussed further following Tax 
Table A. 
The City utilizes its enterprise zone tax abatement program as a means to provide incentive 
for commercial and industrial development.  The tax abatement terms range from 50% to 75% of 
tax abatement on the value of any improvement to real property for period of five to ten years.  
The City currently has ten such tax abatement agreements in eff ect with abatement on 
approximately $4,727,380 of taxable value of real estate.

Tax Rates 
All references to tax rates under this caption are in terms of stated rates in mills per $1.00 
of assessed valuation. 
The Charter provides that the maximum total tax rate that may be levied without a vote of 
the electors for all current operating expenses is 3.27 mills; provided that said 3.27 mills must be 
reduced in each year by that number of mills (or fractional part thereof) which is levied within the 
ten-mill limitation for the purpose of paying debt service on unvoted general obligation bonds and 
bond anticipation notes of the City.  The City has not levied o r collected an ad valorem property 
tax for unvoted debt service purpo ses in at least the last 25 y ears.  In addition, as noted under  
- 47 - 
MUNICIPAL INCOME TAX, a 1975 voter approved ordinance provides for a formula reduction 
of the City’s ad valorem tax rate based on the rate at which in come taxes are collected.  By an 
elector initiated Charter amendment approved May 8, 1990 an additional ad valorem property tax 
of 3.00 mills for five years commencing with collection year 19 91 may be used only to pay for 
additional police staffing and certain related costs as directe d by the Charter amendment.  At an 
election held on May 8, 2001 the tax was renewed at a rate of 3.00 mills commencing tax collection 
year 2002, to remain in effect until amended or repealed by a majority of electors.  The tax levy is 
in addition to the 3.27 mills and is not subject to the aforesaid reduction provisions.

Statutory procedures limit, by the application of tax credits, the amount realized by each 
taxing subdivision from real pr operty taxation to the amount re alized from those taxes in the 
preceding year plus both: 
 the proceeds of any new taxes (other than renewals) approved by  t h e  e l e c t o r s ,  
calculated to produce an amount equal to the amount that would have been realized 
if those taxes had been levied in the preceding year; and 
 amounts realized from new and existing taxes on the assessed va luation of real 
property added to the tax duplicate since the preceding year. 
These procedures were instituted initially in 1976 [Section 319.301 of the Revised Code 
(HB 920 and later acts); Ohio Const. Art. XII, Sec. 2a]  to limit in part the effect of increasing 
property values on the growth of those property taxes.  
- 50 - 
As noted above, all of the City’s property tax levies, as levies inside the ten-mill limitation 
or as Charter tax rates, are exempt from those tax credit provi sions.  The tax credit provisions do 
not apply to amounts realized from taxes levied at whatever rate is required to produce a specified 
amount or an amount to pay debt charges, or from taxes levied inside the ten-mill limitation or any 
applicable charter tax rate limitation.  To calculate the limit ed amount to be realized, a reduction 
factor is applied to the stated rates of the levies subject to these tax credits.  A resulting “effective 
tax rate” reflects the aggregate of those reductions, and is th e rate on the basis of which real 
property taxes are in fact collected.  As an example, the total overlapping tax rate for the 2019 tax 
collection year of 88.61 mills within the City (in that portion  of the City within the Springfield 
City School District) is reduced  by reduction factors of 0.2792 13 for residential/agricultural 
property and 0.171770 for all other real property, which result s in “effective tax rates” of 
63.876147 mills for residential and agricultural property and 7 3.397794 mills for all other real 
property.  See Tax Table A. 
Residential and agricultural r eal property tax amounts paid by taxpayers generally have 
been further reduced by an additional 10% (12.5% in the case of  owner-occupied residential 
property); however, legislation pa ssed by the State’s General A ssembly in 2013 eliminated such 
reductions for additional and replacement levies approved at elections after its effective date and 
for other taxes (or increases in taxes) not levied for tax year  2013.  See AD VALOREM 
PROPERTY TAXES AND SPECIAL ASSESSMENTS – Collections  for a discussion of 
reimbursements by the State to taxing subdivisions for these reductions and related changes made 
by that State legislation.

Included in the “Current Billed” and “Current Collected” figures above are payments made 
from State revenue sources under two statewide real property tax relief programs, the Homestead 
Exemption and the Property Tax Rollback Exemption.  The Homestead Exemption is available for 
(i) persons 65 years of age or older, (ii) persons who are tota lly or permanently disabled and 
(iii) surviving spouses of persons who were totally or permanently disabled or 65 years of age or 
older, and had applied and qualified for a reduction of property taxes in the year of death, so long 
as the surviving spouses were not younger than 59 or older than 65 years of age on the date of their 
deceased spouses’ deaths.  The Homestead Exemption exempts $25,000 of the homestead’s market 
value from taxation, thereby reducing the property owner’s ad valorem property tax liability.  The 
Property Tax Rollback Exemption a pplies to all non-business pro perties, and reduces each 
property owner’s ad valorem property tax liability by either 12 .5% (for owner-occupied non-
business properties) or 10% (for non-owner non-business occupie d properties).  Payments to  
- 52 - 
taxing subdivisions have been made in amounts approximately equ al to the Homestead and 
Property Tax Rollback Exemptions granted.  This State assistanc e reflected in the City’s tax 
collections for 2018 was $146,097 for the elderly/disabled home stead payment and $164,526 for 
the rollback payment (excluding payments for the Springfield Conservancy District). 
Legislation passed by the State’s General Assembly in 2013 made  t h e  H o m e s t e a d  
Exemption subject to means testing beginning January 1, 2014, a nd eliminated the Property Tax 
Rollback Exemption and related reimbursements with respect to n ew or replacement tax levies 
approved at elections after its effective date, and for other taxes (or increases in taxes) not levied 
for tax year 2013.  See AD VALOREM PROPERTY TAXES AND SPECIAL 
ASSESSMENTS – Tax Rates. 
Real property taxes are payable in two installments, the first usually by February and the 
second in July. 
Special Assessments 
The City regularly conducts residential and other street improvements, which can include 
paving, resurfacing, draining, plan ting shade trees and constru cting curbs, sidewalks, storm 
sewers, sanitary sewers and water lines.  The cost of these imp rovements is paid in part from 
special assessments levied against the property benefiting from those improvements; the remaining 
cost is paid by the City.  Unless all of the benefiting property owners petition to pay all costs, State 
law requires the City to pay at least 2% (plus the cost associa ted with intersections) of the total 
cost of the improvements. 
Owners of benefiting properties ma y commence a street improveme nt project by filing a 
petition with the Commission requ esting the improvement.  Alter natively, the Commission, with 
a three-quarter majority, may by resolution declare the necessi ty for such an improvement.  The 
special assessment proceedings pro vide for notice to property o wners and an opportunity for 
property owners to object to the special assessments.  At the commencement of construction of the 
improvement, bond anticipation notes may be issued to pay the p roperty owners’ portion of the 
project cost.  Following comple tion of the work and determinati on of final costs, the special 
assessments are levied by the Commission against the benefiting  property.  Special assessments 
not paid within 30 days are certified to the County Auditor for  collection over a period of time 
(usually 10 to 20 years for most projects).  Special assessment s are billed by the County Auditor 
and collected by the County Treasurer along with and at the same time as real property taxes.  The 
real property taxes levied on any property against which special assessments have been levied are 
not to be paid unless those special assessments are also paid. 
If bond anticipation notes are issued to pay the property owner’s portion of the project cost 
as described above, bonds may be issued in anticipation of the collection of the special assessments 
to refund (together with any cash payments of special assessmen ts) those notes.  The special 
assessments certified for collection bear the same interest as the bonds.  Under State law, those 
bonds are to be paid from the anticipated special assessments, but they are also general obligations 
of the City, payable from ad valorem property taxes to the exte nt not paid from those special 
assessments.  See CITY DEBT AND OTHER LONG-TER M OBLIGATIONS – Statutory 
Direct Debt Limitations, – Indirect Debt and Unvoted Property T ax Limitations and Debt  
- 53 - 
Tables A and B.  The City has never been required to levy an ad valorem prope rty tax for debt 
charges on bonds issued in anticipation of the collection of sp ecial assessments because special 
assessments have been collected as required and sufficient bala nces have been available in the 
Bond Retirement Fund to cover any temporary shortfall.

Delinquencies 
The following is a general description of delinquency procedure s under Ohio law, the 
implementation of which may vary in practice among the counties. 
Under the Revised Code, taxes levied on property become a lien of the State on the first 
day of January, annually, and that lien continues until the tax es, including any penalties, interest 
or other charges, are paid.  Real estate taxes and special assessments are generally payable in two 
equal installments – with the first half installment due no lat er than January in the year following 
their levy and the second half installment due no later than Ju ly in the year following their levy.  
Current year billed taxes not paid when due are generally subje ct to a 10% penalty, subject to 
remission of one-half of the penalty if those taxes are paid within 10 days following the due date. 
The county auditor is required to prepare a list and duplicate of delinquent lands 
(“delinquent lands duplicate”) immediately after each settlement with the county treasurer of taxes 
collected and to certify the de linquent lands duplicate to the county treasurer.  Taxes, including 
penalties, interest and other ch arges, that remain unpaid after  the last day that the second half 
installment of current taxes may be paid without penalty in any  year become “delinquent taxes” 
and the properties to which they relate comprise the delinquent lands duplicate.  If delinquent taxes 
(and special assessments) are not paid within 60 days after a c opy of the county auditor’s 
delinquent land duplicate is delivered to the county treasurer, then the county treasurer is to enforce 
the lien of the State that attached on January 1 of the year the taxes were levied.  Under State law, 
the county treasurer is to enforce the lien “in the same way mo rtgage liens are enforced,” that is, 
by an action in the court of common pleas for foreclosure and s ale of the property in satisfaction 
of the delinquency.  If the county treasurer fails to bring an action to enforce the lien, then the State 
Tax Commissioner is to do so.  I n addition, one year after cert ification of a delinquent land 
duplicate, the county prosecuting attorney is authorized to institute foreclosure proceedings in the 
name of the county treasurer to foreclose the lien.  
- 54 - 
The property owner may execute a written delinquent tax agreeme nt with the county 
treasurer to arrange a payment plan that provides for payments of tax, interest, and penalties over 
a period not to exceed five years.  If payments are made when d ue under the plan, no further 
interest will be assessed agai nst delinquent balances covered b y the plan; however, a default in 
any payment under the plan or in the payment of current taxes w ill invalidate the taxpayer’s 
participation in the plan.  If a payment plan is not adhered to  or if none is arranged, foreclosure 
proceedings may be initiated by the county.  Mass foreclosure proceedings and sales are permitted 
after three years’ delinquency.  Proceeds from delinquent prope rty foreclosure sales become part 
of and are distributed as current collections to the taxing subdivisions. 
As required by law, the County deposits 5% of all collections of certified delinquent taxes 
and assessments into the delinquent real estate tax and assessment collection fund.  Money in that 
fund is divided equally between the County Treasurer and Prosecuting Attorney and is used solely 
in connection with the collection of delinquent taxes and assessments. 
In recent years, the State legislature has enacted several prog r a m s  w i t h  r e s p e c t  t o  
forestalling the foreclosure proc ess or the forfeiture of prope rty due to tax delinquency that may 
have the effect of delaying or eliminating the collection of certain property taxes.  Notwithstanding 
the delay or loss of the tax revenues from those properties, an issuer of general obligation notes or 
bonds, such as the City, remains obligated to pay the debt char ges on those notes or bonds from 
the available revenues.  See CITY DEBT AND OTHER LONG-TERM OBLIGATIONS – 
Security for General Obligation Debt; Bonds and BANs. 
Of the 27,354 nonexempt parcels in the City for collection year  2018, the number of 
delinquent parcels was 350, against 199 of which foreclosure proceedings were commenced. 
There is no one taxpayer that accounted for more than 5% of any  of the billed taxes or 
accumulated delinquencies, shown in the table above for tax collection year 2018. 
MUNICIPAL INCOME TAX 
Ohio law authorizes a city or village to levy a municipal incom e tax on both business 
income and employee wages and salaries at a rate of up to 1% wi thout voter authorization, first 
effective July 1, 1948, however, the Charter limits the tax rate (absent a Charter amendment) to a 
maximum rate of 2.50%. 
The current rate of 2.40% consists of 1.50% that will continue indefinitely unless reduced 
or increased, but subject to the present Charter limit, by voter action.  An additional 0.50% would 
have expired on June 30, 2015; however, at an election on May 6 , 2014, the voters of the City 
renewed this 0.50% for an additional 15 years, to expire on Jun e 30, 2030.  At an election on 
May 2, 2017, the voters of the City approved an additional 0.40 % effective July 1, 2017 through 
December 31, 2022.  This tax on business income and individuals’ salaries and wages is collected 
and administered by the City. 
Actions taken in 1975 with respect to the income tax have resul ted in a roll back of the 
Charter authorized property tax of 3.27 mills to zero mills through June 30, 2000.  The renewal of 
the 0.5% income tax rate also extended the rollback of the prop erty tax to zero mills through 
June 30, 2030.  
- 55 - 
Because of the relationship between the 1975 income tax authorizations and the provision 
for the reduction of the rate of ad valorem taxes, the City, in the event it were to levy an ad valorem 
tax for debt service or other m unicipal purposes would probably  be required to reduce the 
municipal income tax from the presently authorized rate of 2.40% to 1.90%. 
The municipal income tax is a tax on net profits from a busines s or profession, exclusive 
of income from tangible personal property, and on payment for work done or services performed, 
including wages, salaries, commissions and other compensation.  The municipal income tax is 
subject to termination by action of the Commission or an election that could be initiated by petition 
of 5% of the registered voters in  the City at the last regular municipal election.  A reduction or 
termination of the municipal income tax would then have to be approved by a majority vote of the 
electors of the City.  The Commission could reimpose a 1% tax w ithout authorization by the 
electors, as long as the Charter continues to authorize the lev y in such amount or in a higher 
amount. 
The municipal income tax is collected and administered by the Income Tax Division of the 
City’s Finance Department. In November 1982, City voters approv ed an amendment to City 
ordinances authorizing the City to require monthly, rather than  quarterly, payment of income tax 
withholdings of employees within the City.  Expenses of collect ion are paid from the City’s 
General Fund. 
Income tax proceeds, after payment of collection expenses, have  been allocated by the 
Commission for 2019 as follows:  10% to capital expenditures, including payment of debt charges, 
and 90% to the General Fund.

$31,000 was charged off in 2018.

tax.
